804\'\$\'NOTE\\nThe\ first\ publication\ of\ the\ literary\ work\ of\ the\\nSisters\ Bronte\ was\,\ as\ we\ know\,\ the\ small\ volume\ of\\npoems\ which\ Messrs.\ Aylott\ and\ Jones\ issued\ in\\n1846.\ That\ year\ must\ have\ been\ a\ busy\ and\ trying\\none\ to\ the\ three\ lonely\ and\ ambitious\ girls\ in\ the\\nsecluded\ Haworth\ parsonage\,\ then\ for\ the\ first\ time\\nin\ living\ relationship\ with\ the\ busy\ literary\ world\ of\\nLondon.\ In\ a\ letter\ to\ Messrs.\ Aylott\ and\ Jones\,\\nunder\ date\ April\ 6th\,\ and\ over\ the\ signature\,\ \"C.\\nBronte\,\"\ the\ publishers\ were\ informed\ that\ \"C\,\ E.\,\\nand\ A.\ Bell\ are\ now\ preparing\ for\ the\ press\ a\ work\\nof\ fiction\,\ consisting\ of\ three\ distinct\ and\ uncon-\\nnected\ tales.\"\ It\ was\ suggested\ by\ the\ writer\ that\\nthese\ tales\ might\ \"be\ published\ either\ together\,\ as\\na\ work\ of\ three\ volumes\,\ of\ the\ ordinary\ novel\ size\,\\nor\ separately\ as\ single\ volumes.\"\ The\ three\ tales\\nwere\ \"Wuthering\ Heights\,\"\ \"Agnes\ Grey\,\"\ and\\n\"The\ Professor.\"\ The\ two\ by\ Emily\ and\ Anne\\nfound\ a\ publisher\ in\ the\ person\ of\ Newby\,\ of\\nMortimer\ Street.\ \"The\ Professor\,\"\ however\,\ was\\nrejected\ again\ and\ again\,\ and\ it\ was\ only\ until\ two\\nv\'\$\'NOTE\\nyears\ after\ its\ author\\\'s\ death\ that\ it\ was\ issued\ to\\na\ public\ ready\ to\ read\ any\ work\ from\ the\ writer\ of\\n\"Jane\ Eyre\"\ and\ \"Shirley\,\"\ by\ the\ very\ firm\ that\\nrefused\ it\ eleven\ years\ before.\\nWe\ can\ imagine\ what\ Charlotte\ Bronte\ must\ have\\nfelt\ each\ time\ \"The\ Professor\"\ came\ back\ to\ her\\nas\ we\ read\ what\ she\ herself\ has\ said:\ \"Currer\\nBell\\\'s\ book\ found\ acceptance\ nowhere\,\ nor\ any\\nacknowledgment\ of\ merit\,\ so\ that\ something\ like\\nthe\ chill\ of\ despair\ began\ to\ invade\ his\ heart.\"\ Not\\na\ peculiar\ experience\ this\,\ but\ the\ despair\ did\ not\\ndiscourage\ her.\ In\ spite\ of\ her\ disappointment\,\ in\\nspite\ of\ the\ home\ troubles\ and\ their\ consequent\\nanxietiesâ\\200\\224her\ father\\\'s\ threatened\ blindness\ and\ the\\nbad\ health\ of\ her\ sistersâ\\200\\224she\ began\ \"Jane\ Eyre.\"\\nAnd\ as\ if\ disappointment\,\ trouble\,\ house-care\,\ and\\nwork\ were\ not\ enough\,\ the\ year\ must\ end\ with\\na\ kind\ of\ emphasis\ of\ pain\ in\ the\ arrival\ of\ a\\nsheriff\\\'s\ officer\ who\ came\ \"to\ visit\ B.\,\ inviting\ him\\neither\ to\ pay\ his\ debts\ or\ take\ a\ trip\ to\ York.\"\\nTruly\ the\ life\ of\ this\ remarkable\ genius\,\ common-\\nplace\ and\ sordid\ as\ was\ the\ atmosphere\ it\ breathed\,\\ngrows\ more\ and\ more\ astonishing\ in\ our\ sight\,\ the\\nmore\ we\ view\ it\ in\ relation\ to\ the\ work\ she\\naccomplished.\ Nowhere\,\ perhaps\,\ in\ the\ history\\nof\ literature\ can\ we\ find\ a\ parallel\ to\ itâ\\200\\224so\ full\ of\\njust\ the\ very\ trials\ which\ touch\ most\ nearly\ a\\nwoman\\\'s\ heart\;\ and\ yet\ so\ distinguished\ by\ the\\nabsolute\ assertion\ and\ masterly\ achievement\ of\ the\\nartistic\ spirit.\ Only\ by\ means\ of\ the\ sustaining\'\$\'NOTE\\npower\ of\ the\ peculiar\ joy\ which\ comes\ in\ doing\,\ and\\nin\ the\ contemplation\ of\ the\ results\ of\ the\ creative\\nactivity\,\ could\ such\ a\ life\ be\ lived\ at\ all\,\ or\,\ at\ any\\nrate\,\ find\ courage\ for\ each\ day\\\'s\ morrow.\\nIn\ October\,\ 1847\,\ \"Jane\ Eyre\"\ was\ published\,\\nand\ its\ success\ brought\ Charlotte\ Bronte\ inquiries\\nfrom\ her\ publisher\ for\ more\ work.\ In\ a\ letter\ to\\nMr.\ W.\ S.\ Williams\,\ dated\ December\ 14th\ in\ the\\nsame\ year\,\ writing\ in\ reply\ to\ such\ inquiry\,\ she\\nrefers\ again\ to\ the\ rejected\ story:\\n\"Respecting\ the\ plan\ of\ such\ a\ work\,\ I\ have\\npondered\ it\,\ but\ as\ yet\ with\ very\ unsatisfactory\\nresults.\ Three\ commencements\ have\ I\ essayed\,\ but\\nall\ three\ displease\ me.\ A\ few\ days\ since\ I\ looked\\nover\ \\\'The\ Professor.\\\'\ I\ found\ the\ beginning\ very\\nfeeble\,\ the\ whole\ narrative\ deficient\ in\ interest\ and\\nin\ general\ attractiveness.\ Yet\ the\ middle\ and\\nlatter\ portion\ of\ the\ work\,\ all\ that\ relates\ to\\nBrussels\,\ the\ Belgian\ school\,\ etc.\,\ is\ as\ good\ as\ I\\ncan\ write:\ it\ contains\ more\ pith\,\ more\ substance\,\\nmore\ reality\,\ in\ my\ judgment\,\ than\ much\ of\ \\\'Jane\\nEyre.\\\'\ It\ gives\,\ I\ think\,\ a\ new\ view\ of\ a\ grade\,\ an\\noccupation\,\ and\ a\ class\ of\ charactersâ\\200\\224all\ very\\ncommonplace\,\ very\ insignificant\ in\ themselves\,\ but\\nnot\ more\ so\ than\ the\ materials\ composing\ that\\nportion\ of\ \\\'Jane\ Eyre\\\'\ which\ seems\ to\ please\\nmost\ generally.\\n\"My\ wish\ is\ to\ recast\ \\\'The\ Professor\,\\\'\ add\ as\\nwell\ as\ I\ can\ what\ is\ deficient\,\ retouch\ some\ parts\,\\ndevelop\ others\,\ and\ make\ of\ it\ a\ three-volume\ work\\nvii\'\$\'NOTE\\n/\\nâ\\200\\224no\ easy\ task\,\ I\ know\,\ yet\ I\ trust\ not\ an\\nimpracticable\ one.\"\\nThe\ recasting\ of\ \"The\ Professor\"\ resulted\ in\\n\"Villette.\"\ Still\,\ the\ original\ story\,\ as\ it\ now\\nstands\,\ deserves\ a\ place\ to\ itself.\ If\ its\ present\\nform\ be\ the\ form\ in\ which\ it\ went\ the\ round\ of\ the\\npublishers\,\ its\ rejection\ must\ have\ been\ due\ to\ the\\nfact\ that\ it\ would\ not\ furnish\ the\ regulation\ three\\nvolumes\,\ and\ not\ to\ any\ lack\ of\ intrinsic\ worth.\\nCharlotte\ Bronte\\\'s\ own\ opinion\ of\ its\ merits\ is\ an\\nextremely\ sane\ one\,\ and\ she\ possessed\ in\ no\ slight\\ndegree\,\ the\ power\ to\ judge\ her\ own\ work\ dis-\\npassionately.\\nWe\ learn\ from\ Mr.\ Clement\ K.\ Shorter\\\'s\\n\"Charlotte\ Bronte\ and\ her\ Circle\,\"\ that\ Mrs.\\nGaskell\ was\ to\ have\ edited\ and\ revised\ \"The\\nProfessor\"\ for\ publication\;\ but\ this\ the\ Rev.\ A.\ B.\\nNicholls\ refused\ to\ permit\,\ and\ the\ story\ was\ issued\\nin\ two\ volumes\ in\ 1857\,\ prefixed\ by\ the\ short\ note\\nby\ Mr.\ Nicholls\ himself.\\nTemple\ Scott.\\nviu\'\$\'PREFACE\\nThis\ little\ book\ was\ written\ before\ either\ \"Jane\\nEyre\ \"\ or\ \"Shirley\,\"\ and\ yet\ no\ indulgence\ can\ be\\nsolicited\ for\ it\ on\ the\ plea\ of\ a\ first\ attempt.\ A\\nfirst\ attempt\ it\ certainly\ was\ not\,\ as\ the\ pen\ which\\nwrote\ it\ had\ been\ previously\ worn\ a\ good\ deal\ in\ a\\npractice\ of\ some\ years.\ I\ had\ not\ indeed\ published\\nanything\ before\ I\ commenced\ \"The\ Professor\,\"\ but\\nin\ many\ a\ crude\ effort\,\ destroyed\ almost\ as\ soon\ as\\ncomposed\,\ I\ had\ got\ over\ any\ such\ taste\ as\ I\ might\\nonce\ have\ had\ for\ ornamented\ and\ redundant\\ncomposition\,\ and\ come\ to\ prefer\ what\ was\ plain\ and\\nhomely.\ At\ the\ same\ time\ I\ had\ adopted\ a\ set\\nof\ principles\ on\ the\ subject\ of\ incident\,\ etc.\,\ such\\nas\ would\ be\ generally\ approved\ in\ theory\,\ but\ the\\nresult\ of\ which\,\ when\ carried\ out\ into\ practice\,\\noften\ procures\ for\ an\ author\ more\ surprise\ than\\npleasure.\\nI\ said\ to\ myself\ that\ my\ hero\ should\ work\ his\\nway\ through\ life\ as\ I\ had\ seen\ real\ living\ men\ work\\ntheirsâ\\200\\224that\ he\ should\ never\ get\ a\ shilling\ he\ had\\nnot\ earnedâ\\200\\224that\ no\ sudden\ turns\ should\ lift\ him\ in\\na\ moment\ to\ wealth\ and\ high\ station\;\ that\ whatever\\nix\'\$\'PREFACE\\nsmall\ competency\ he\ might\ gain\,\ should\ be\ won\ by\\nthe\ sweat\ of\ his\ brow\;\ that\,\ before\ he\ could\ find\ so\\nmuch\ as\ an\ arbour\ to\ sit\ down\ in\,\ he\ should\ master\\nat\ least\ half\ the\ ascent\ of\ \"\ the\ Hill\ of\ Difficulty\ \"\;\\nthat\ he\ should\ not\ even\ marry\ a\ beautiful\ girl\ or\ a\\nlady\ of\ rank.\ As\ Adam\\\'s\ son\ he\ should\ share\\nAdam\\\'s\ doom\,\ and\ drain\ throughout\ life\ a\ mixed\\nand\ moderate\ cup\ of\ enjoyment.\\nIn\ the\ sequel\,\ however\,\ I\ found\ that\ publishers\\nin\ general\ scarcely\ approved\ of\ this\ system\,\ but\\nwould\ have\ liked\ something\ more\ imaginative\ and\\npoeticalâ\\200\\224something\ more\ consonant\ with\ a\ highly\\nwrought\ fancy\,\ with\ a\ taste\ for\ pathos\,\ with\ senti-\\nments\ more\ tender\,\ elevated\,\ unworldly.\ Indeed\\nuntil\ an\ author\ has\ tried\ to\ dispose\ of\ a\ manuscript\\nof\ this\ kind\,\ he\ can\ never\ know\ what\ stores\ of\\nromance\ and\ sensibility\ lie\ hidden\ in\ breasts\ he\\nwould\ not\ have\ suspected\ of\ casketing\ such\\ntreasures.\ Men\ in\ business\ are\ usually\ thought\ to\\nprefer\ the\ real\;\ on\ trial\ the\ idea\ will\ be\ often\ found\\nfallacious:\ a\ passionate\ preference\ for\ the\ wild\,\\nwonderful\,\ and\ thrillingâ\\200\\224the\ strange\,\ startling\,\ and\\nharrowingâ\\200\\224agitates\ divers\ souls\ that\ show\ a\ calm\\nand\ sober\ surface.\\nSuch\ being\ the\ case\,\ the\ reader\ will\ comprehend\\nthat\ to\ have\ reached\ him\ in\ the\ form\ of\ a\ printed\\nbook\,\ this\ brief\ narrative\ must\ have\ gone\ through\\nsome\ strugglesâ\\200\\224which\ indeed\ it\ has.\ And\ after\ all\,\\nits\ worst\ struggle\ and\ strongest\ ordeal\ is\ yet\ to\ come\;\\nbut\ it\ takes\ comfortâ\\200\\224subdues\ fearâ\\200\\224leans\ on\ the\'\$\'PREFACE\\nstaff\ of\ a\ moderate\ expectationâ\\200\\224and\ mutters\ under\\nits\ breath\,\ while\ lifting\ its\ eye\ to\ that\ of\ the\\npublic\,\\n\"He\ that\ is\ low\ need\ fear\ no\ fall.\"\\nCurrer\ Bell.\\nThe\ foregoing\ preface\ was\ written\ by\ my\ wife\\nwith\ a\ view\ to\ the\ publication\ of\ \"The\ Professor\,\"\\nshortly\ after\ the\ appearance\ of\ \"Shirley.\"\ Being\\ndissuaded\ from\ her\ intention\,\ the\ authors\ made\\nsome\ use\ of\ the\ materials\ in\ a\ subsequent\ workâ\\200\\224\\n\"Villette.\"\ As\,\ however\,\ these\ two\ stories\ are\ in\\nmost\ respects\ unlike\,\ it\ has\ been\ represented\ to\\nme\ that\ I\ ought\ not\ to\ withhold\ \"The\ Professor\"\\nfrom\ the\ public.\ I\ have\ therefore\ consented\ to\ its\\npublication.\\nA\ B.\ NicnoLLS.\\nJIa\ worth\ Parsonage\,\\nSeptember\ 22nd\,\ 185ft\'\'\'\$\'LIST\ OF\ ILLUSTRATIONS\\ncharlotte\ bronâ\\204¢\ ....\ Frontispiece\\nFrom\ a\ Portrait\ by\ O.\ I.\\nINTRODUCTORY.\\nThe\ other\ day\,\ in\ looking\ over\ my\ papers\,\ I\ found\\nin\ my\ desk\ the\ following\ copy\ of\ a\ letter\ sent\ by\\nme\ a\ year\ since\ to\ an\ old\ school\ acquaintance\ :â\\200\\224\\n\"Dear\ Charles\,â\\200\\224I\ think\ when\ you\ and\ I\ were\\nat\ Eton\ together\,\ we\ were\ neither\ of\ us\ what\ could\\nbe\ called\ popular\ characters:\ you\ were\ a\ sarcastic\,\\nobservant\,\ shrewd\,\ cold-blooded\ creature\;\ my\ own\\nportrait\ I\ will\ not\ attempt\ to\ draw\,\ but\ I\ cannot\\nrecollect\ that\ it\ was\ a\ strikingly\ attractive\ oneâ\\200\\224can\\nyou\?\ What\ animal\ magnetism\ drew\ thee\ and\ me\\ntogether\,\ I\ know\ not\;\ certainly\ I\ never\ experienced\\nanything\ of\ the\ Pylades\ and\ Orestes\ sentiment\ for\\nyou\,\ and\ I\ have\ reason\ to\ believe\ that\ you\,\ on\ your\\npart\,\ were\ equally\ free\ from\ all\ romantic\ regard\ to\\nme.\ Still\,\ out\ of\ school\ hours\,\ we\ walked\ and\\ntalked\ continually\ together\;\ when\ the\ theme\ of\\nconversation\ was\ our\ companions\ or\ our\ masters\ we\\nI\ B\'\$\'THE\ PROFESSOR\\nunderstood\ each\ other\,\ and\ when\ I\ recurred\ to\\nsome\ sentiment\ of\ affection\,\ some\ vague\ love\ of\ ar\\nexcellent\ or\ beautiful\ object\,\ whether\ in\ animate\ or\\ninanimate\ nature\,\ your\ sardonic\ coldness\ did\ not\\nmove\ me.\ I\ felt\ myself\ superior\ to\ that\ check\ then\\nas\ I\ do\ now.\\n\"It\ is\ a\ long\ time\ since\ I\ wrote\ to\ you\,\ and\ a\\nstill\ longer\ time\ since\ I\ saw\ you.\ Chancing\ to\ take\\nup\ a\ newspaper\ of\ your\ county\ the\ other\ day\,\ my\\neye\ fell\ upon\ your\ name.\ I\ began\ to\ think\ of\ old\\ntimes\;\ to\ run\ over\ the\ events\ which\ have\ transpired\\nsince\ we\ separated\;\ and\ I\ sat\ down\ and\ commenced\\nthis\ letter.\ What\ you\ have\ been\ doing\,\ I\ know\ not\;\\nbut\ you\ shall\ hear\,\ if\ you\ choose\ to\ listen\,\ how\ the\\nworld\ has\ wagged\ with\ me.\\n\"First\,\ after\ leaving\ Eton\,\ I\ had\ an\ interview\\nwith\ my\ maternal\ uncles\,\ Lord\ Tynedale\ and\ the\\nHon.\ John\ Seacombe.\ They\ asked\ me\ if\ I\ would\\nenter\ the\ Church\,\ and\ my\ uncle\ the\ nobleman\\noffered\ me\ the\ living\ of\ Seacombe\,\ which\ is\ in\ his\\ngift\,\ if\ I\ would\;\ then\ my\ other\ uncle\,\ Mr.\ Seacombe\,\\nhinted\ that\ when\ I\ beoame\ rector\ of\ Seacombe-cum-\\nScaife\,\ I\ might\ perhaps\ be\ allowed\ to\ take\,\ as\\nmistress\ of\ my\ house\ and\ head\ of\ my\ parish\,\ one\\nof\ my\ six\ cousins\,\ his\ daughters\,\ all\ of\ whom\ I\\ngreatly\ dislike.\\n\"I\ declined\ both\ the\ Church\ and\ matrimony.\ A\\ngood\ clergyman\ is\ a\ good\ thing\,\ but\ I\ should\ have\\nmade\ a\ very\ bad\ one.\ As\ to\ the\ wifeâ\\200\\224oh\,\ how\ like\\na\ nightmare\ is\ the\ thought\ of\ being\ bound\ for\ life\\n2\'\$\'THE\ PROFESSOR\\nto\ one\ of\ my\ cousins\!\ No\ doubt\ they\ are\ accom-\\nplished\ and\ pretty\;\ but\ not\ an\ accomplishment\,\\nnot\ a\ charm\ of\ theirs\,\ touches\ a\ chord\ in\ my\ bosom.\\nTo\ think\ of\ passing\ the\ winter\ evenings\ by\ the\\nparlour\ fireside\ of\ Seacombe\ Rectory\ alone\ with\ one\\nof\ themâ\\200\\224for\ instance\,\ the\ large\ and\ well-modelled\\nstatue\,\ Sarahâ\\200\\224no\;\ I\ should\ be\ a\ bad\ husband\,\\nunder\ such\ circumstances\,\ as\ well\ as\ a\ bad\ clergy-\\nman.\\n\"When\ I\ had\ declined\ my\ uncles\\\'\ offers\ they\\nasked\ me\ \\\'what\ I\ intended\ to\ do.\\\'\ I\ said\ I\ should\\nreflect.\ They\ reminded\ me\ that\ I\ had\ no\ fortune\,\\nand\ no\ expectation\ of\ any\,\ and\,\ after\ a\ considerable\\npause\,\ Lord\ Tynedale\ demanded\ sternly\,\ \*\ Whether\\nI\ had\ thoughts\ of\ following\ my\ father\\\'s\ steps\ and\\nengaging\ in\ trade\?\\\'\ Now\,\ I\ had\ had\ no\ thoughts\\nof\ the\ sort.\ I\ do\ not\ think\ that\ my\ turn\ of\ mind\\nqualifies\ me\ to\ make\ a\ good\ tradesman\;\ my\ taste\,\\nmy\ ambition\ does\ not\ lie\ that\ way\;\ but\ such\ was\\nthe\ scorn\ expressed\ in\ Lord\ Tynedale\\\'s\ countenance\\nas\ he\ pronounced\ the\ word\ tradeâ\\200\\224such\ the\ con-\\ntemptuous\ sarcasm\ of\ his\ toneâ\\200\\224that\ I\ was\ instantly\\ndecided.\ My\ father\ was\ but\ a\ name\ to\ me\,\ yet\ that\\nname\ I\ did\ not\ like\ to\ hear\ mentioned\ with\ a\ sneer\\nto\ my\ very\ face.\ I\ answered\ then\,\ with\ haste\ and\\nwarmth\,\ \\\'I\ cannot\ do\ better\ than\ follow\ in\ my\\nfather\\\'s\ steps\;\ yes\,\ I\ will\ be\ a\ tradesman.\\\'\ My\\nuncles\ did\ not\ remonstrate\;\ they\ and\ I\ parted\ with\\nmutual\ disgust.\ In\ reviewing\ this\ transaction\ I\\nfind\ that\ I\ was\ quite\ right\ to\ shake\ off\ the\ burden\\ni\'\$\'THE\ PROFESSOR\\nof\ Tynedale\\\'s\ patronage\,\ but\ a\ fool\ to\ offer\ my\\nshoulders\ instantly\ for\ the\ reception\ of\ another\\nburdenâ\\200\\224one\ which\ might\ be\ more\ intolerable\,\ and\\nwhich\ certainly\ was\ yet\ untried.\\n\"I\ wrote\ instantly\ to\ Edwardâ\\200\\224you\ know\ Edward\\nâ\\200\\224my\ only\ brother\,\ ten\ years\ my\ senior\,\ married\ to\\na\ rich\ millowner\\\'s\ daughter\,\ and\ now\ possessor\ of\\nthe\ mill\ and\ business\ which\ was\ my\ father\\\'s\ before\\nhe\ failed.\ You\ are\ aware\ that\ my\ fatherâ\\200\\224once\\nreckoned\ a\ Croesus\ of\ wealthâ\\200\\224became\ bankrupt\ a\\nshort\ time\ previous\ to\ his\ death\,\ and\ that\ my\ mother\\nlived\ in\ destitution\ for\ some\ six\ months\ after\ him\,\\nunhelped\ by\ her\ aristocratical\ brothers\,\ whom\ she\\nhad\ mortally\ offended\ by\ her\ union\ with\ Crimsworth.\\nthe\ shire\ manufacturer.\ At\ the\ end\ of\ the\\nsix\ months\ she\ brought\ me\ into\ the\ world\,\ and\\nthen\ herself\ left\ it\ without\,\ I\ should\ think\,\ much\\nregret\,\ as\ it\ contained\ little\ hope\ or\ comfort\ for\\nher.\\n\"My\ father\\\'s\ relations\ took\ charge\ of\ Edward\,\ as\\nthey\ did\ of\ me\ till\ I\ was\ nine\ years\ old.\ At\ that\\nperiod\ it\ chanced\ that\ the\ representation\ of\ an\\nimportant\ borough\ in\ our\ county\ fell\ vacant\;\ Mr.\\nBeacombe\ stood\ for\ it.\ My\ uncle\ Crimsworth\,\ an\\nastute\,\ mercantile\ man\,\ took\ the\ opportunity\ of\\nwriting\ a\ fierce\ letter\ to\ the\ candidate\,\ stating\ that\\nif\ he\ and\ Lord\ Tynedale\ did\ not\ consent\ to\ do\\nsomething\ towards\ the\ support\ of\ their\ sister\\\'s\\norphan\ children\ he\ would\ expose\ their\ relentless\\nand\ malignant\ conduct\ towards\ that\ sister\,\ and\ do\\n4\'\$\'THE\ PROFESSOR\\nhis\ best\ to\ turn\ the\ circumstances\ against\ Mr.\\nSeacombe\\\'s\ election.\ That\ gentleman\ and\ Lord\\nT.\ knew\ well\ enough\ that\ the\ Crimsworths\ were\ an\\nunscrupulous\ and\ determined\ race\;\ they\ knew\ also\\nthat\ they\ had\ influence\ in\ the\ borough\ of\ X\;\\nand\,\ making\ a\ virtue\ of\ necessity\,\ they\ consented\\nto\ defray\ the\ expenses\ of\ my\ education.\ I\ was\ sent\\nto\ Eton\,\ where\ I\ remained\ ten\ years\,\ during\ which\\nspace\ of\ time\ Edward\ and\ I\ never\ met.\ He\,\ when\\nhe\ grew\ up\,\ entered\ into\ trade\,\ and\ pursued\ his\\ncalling\ with\ such\ diligence\,\ ability\,\ and\ success\,\\nthat\ now\,\ in\ his\ thirtieth\ year\,\ he\ was\ fast\ making\\na\ fortune.\ Of\ this\ I\ was\ apprised\ by\ the\ occasional\\nshort\ letters\ I\ received\ from\ him\ some\ three\ or\ four\\ntimes\ a\ year\;\ which\ said\ letters\ never\ concluded\\nwithout\ some\ expression\ of\ determined\ enmity\\nagainst\ the\ house\ of\ Seacombe\,\ and\ some\ reproach\\nto\ me\ for\ living\,\ as\ he\ said\,\ on\ the\ bounty\ of\ that\\nhouse.\ At\ first\,\ while\ still\ in\ boyhood\,\ I\ could\ not\\nunderstand\ why\,\ as\ I\ had\ no\ parents\,\ I\ should\ not\\nbe\ indebted\ to\ my\ uncles\ Tynedale\ and\ Seacombe\\nfor\ my\ education\;\ but\ as\ I\ grew\ up\,\ and\ heard\ by\\ndegrees\ of\ the\ persevering\ hostility\,\ the\ hatred\ till\\ndeath\ evinced\ by\ them\ against\ my\ fatherâ\\200\\224of\ the\\nsufferings\ of\ my\ motherâ\\200\\224of\ all\ the\ wrongs\,\ in\ short\,\\nof\ our\ houseâ\\200\\224then\ did\ I\ conceive\ shame\ of\ the\\ndependence\ in\ which\ I\ lived\,\ and\ form\ a\ resolution\\nno\ more\ to\ take\ bread\ from\ hands\ which\ had\ refused\\nto\ minister\ to\ the\ necessities\ of\ my\ dying\ mother.\\nIt\ was\ by\ these\ feelings\ I\ was\ influenced\ when\ I\\n5\'\$\'THE\ PROFESSOR\\nrefused\ the\ Rectory\ of\ Seacombe\ and\ the\ union\\nwith\ one\ of\ my\ patrician\ cousins.\\n\"An\ irreparable\ breach\ thus\ being\ effected\\nbetween\ my\ uncles\ and\ myself\,\ I\ wrote\ to\ Edward\;\\ntold\ him\ what\ had\ occurred\,\ and\ informed\ him\ of\\nmy\ intention\ to\ follow\ his\ steps\ and\ be\ a\ tradesman.\\nI\ asked\,\ moreover\,\ if\ he\ could\ give\ me\ employment.\\nHis\ answer\ expressed\ no\ approbation\ of\ my\ conduct\,\\nbut\ he\ said\ I\ might\ come\ down\ to\ shire\,\ if\ I\\nliked\,\ and\ he\ would\ \\\'see\ what\ could\ be\ done\ in\\nthe\ way\ of\ furnishing\ me\ with\ work.\\\'\ I\ \(railroads\ were\ not\\nthen\ in\ existence\)\ I\ arrived\,\ one\ wet\ October\ after-\\nnoon\,\ in\ the\ town\ of\ X\ .\ I\ had\ always\ under-\\nstood\ that\ Edward\ lived\ in\ this\ town\,\ but\ on\\ninquiry\ I\ found\ that\ it\ was\ only\ Mr.\ Crimsworth\\\'s\\nmill\ and\ warehouse\ which\ were\ situated\ in\ the\\nsmoky\ atmosphere\ of\ Bigben\ Close\;\ his\ residence\\nlay\ four\ miles\ out\,\ in\ the\ country.\\n\"It\ was\ late\ in\ the\ evening\ when\ I\ alighted\ at\\nthe\ gates\ of\ the\ habitation\ designated\ to\ me\ as\ my\\nbrother\\\'s.\ As\ I\ advanced\ up\ the\ avenue\,\ I\ could\\nsee\ through\ the\ shades\ of\ twilight\ and\ the\ dark\\ngloomy\ mists\ which\ deepened\ those\ shades\ that\\nthe\ house\ was\ large\ and\ the\ grounds\ surrounding\ it\\nsufficiently\ spacious.\ I\ paused\ a\ moment\ on\ the\\nlawn\ in\ front\,\ and\ leaning\ my\ back\ against\ a\ tall\\n6\'\$\'THE\ PROFESSOR\ .\\ntree\ wmch\ rose\ in\ the\ centre\,\ I\ gazed\ with\ interest\ V\\non\ the\ exterior\ of\ Crimsworth\ Hall.\\n\"\\\'Edward\ is\ rich\,\\\'\ thought\ I\ to\ myself.\ \\\'I\\nbelieved\ him\ to\ be\ doing\ wellâ\\200\\224but\ I\ did\ not\ know\\nhe\ was\ master\ of\ a\ mansion\ like\ this.\\\'\ Cutting\\nshort\ all\ marvelling\,\ speculation\,\ conjecture\,\ etc.\,\\nI\ advanced\ to\ the\ front\ door\ and\ rang.\ A\ manservant\ opened\ itâ\\200\\224I\ announced\ myselfâ\\200\\224he\ relieved\\nme\ of\ my\ wet\ cloak\ and\ carpet-bag\,\ and\ ushered\\nme\ into\ a\ room\ furnished\ as\ a\ library\,\ where\ there\\nwas\ a\ bright\ fire\,\ and\ candles\ burning\
